The negligible quantity of sodium and fluorine in the $(\mathrm{NaF})_{2}\mathrm{UF}_{4}$ fuel concentrate was not included in the calculations.

The atomic concentrations in Region 1 are:

$$
N _ {F} l = \frac {\rho (\text {w t} \% \text {o f F i n s a l t}) N _ {Q}}{\text {a t o m i c w t o f F}} = 4. 4 9 \times 1 0 ^ {2 2} \text {a t o m s / c c}
$$

$$
N _ {N a l} = 1. 1 2 \times 1 0 ^ {2 2} a t o m s / c c
$$

$$
N _ {Z r l} = 8. 4 2 \times 1 0 ^ {2 1} a t o m s / c c
$$

$$
N _ {U l} = \frac {\left(\text {u r a n i u m c o n c .}\right) N _ {Q}}{2 3 5} = 2. 5 6 x 1 0 ^ {1 9} \text {a t o m s / c c @ 0 . 0 l g m / c c}
$$

The total macroscopic absorption cross section,

$$
\begin{array}{l} \Sigma_ {\text {a l l}} = N _ {F 1} \sigma_ {\text {a l}} ^ {F} + N _ {\text {N a l}} \sigma_ {\text {a l}} ^ {\text {N a}} + N _ {\text {Z r l}} \sigma_ {\text {a l}} ^ {\text {Z r}} + N _ {\text {U l}} \sigma_ {\text {a l}} ^ {\text {U}} \\ = 3. 1 2 \times 1 0 ^ {- 4} \mathrm {c m} ^ {- 1} \\ \end{array}
$$

The total macroscopic fission cross section, multiplied by $\nu$ , the average number of neutrons released per fission (2.46 for U-235),

$$
\nu \Sigma_ {f l l} = N _ {U 1} (\nu \sigma_ {f 1} ^ {U}) = 3. 3 5 \times 1 0 ^ {- 4} f i s s i o n n e u t r o n s / c c @ 0. 0 1 g m / c c U c o n c.
$$

The diffusion coefficient for Group 1, Region 1,

$$
\begin{array}{l} D _ {1 1} = \frac {1}{3 \Sigma_ {\mathrm {t r} 1 1}} = \frac {1}{N _ {F} 2 3 \sigma_ {\mathrm {t r l}} ^ {F} + N _ {\mathrm {N a}} 1 3 \sigma_ {\mathrm {t r l}} ^ {N a} + N _ {\mathrm {Z r}} 1 3 \sigma_ {\mathrm {t r l}} ^ {Z r} + N _ {\mathrm {U}} 1 3 \sigma_ {\mathrm {t r l}} ^ {U}} \\ = 0. 9 0 6 \mathrm {c m} \\ \end{array}
$$

The lethargy change in the fast group,

$$
\Delta u _ {1} = \ln \left(\frac {2 x 1 0 ^ {6} e v}{1 0 0 e v}\right) = 9. 9 \text {u n i t s}
$$

The lethargy change in the intermediate group,

$$
\Delta u _ {2} = \ln \left(\frac {1 0 0 \mathrm {e v}}{0 . 5 \mathrm {e v}}\right) = 5. 3 \text {u n i t s}.
$$

The effective removal cross section $(\Sigma_{\mathbf{x}})$ was approximated as follows:

$$
\Delta u = \text {l e t h a r g y}
$$

$$
\xi = \text {a v e r a g e c h a n g e i n l e t h a r g y p e r c o l l i s i o n}
$$

$$
\Sigma_ {x} \cong \frac {\sum_ {s} \phi}{\Delta u / \xi} \quad \frac {\text {a v e r a g e n o . o f s c a t t e r i n g c o l l i s i o n s / c c - s e c}}{\text {a v g n o . o f s c a t t e r i n g c o l l i s i o n s f o r n e u t r o n s t o p a s s t h r u u}}
$$

Approximating $\Sigma_{\mathbf{s}}$ by $\Sigma_{t}$ , the effective removal cross section, or effective cross section, for a neutron to pass entirely through a lethargy interval, $u$ , becomes,

$$
\Sigma_ {x} \cong \frac {\xi \Sigma_ {t}}{\Delta u}
$$

The effective removal cross section for Group 1, Region 1,

$$
\begin{array}{l} \Sigma_ {x 1 1} \cong \frac {\mathrm {N} _ {\mathrm {F}} \mathrm {l} \left(\overline {{\xi \sigma}} _ {\mathrm {t}}\right) \mathrm {F} _ {1} + \mathrm {N} _ {\mathrm {N a l}} \left(\overline {{\xi \sigma}} _ {\mathrm {t}}\right) \mathrm {N a l} + \mathrm {N} _ {\mathrm {Z r} _ {1}} \left(\overline {{\xi \sigma}} _ {\mathrm {t}}\right) \mathrm {Z r} _ {1} + \mathrm {N} _ {\mathrm {U l}} \left(\overline {{\xi \sigma}} _ {\mathrm {t}}\right) \mathrm {U l}}{\Delta u _ {1}} \\ = 3. 0 4 \times 1 0 ^ {- 3} \mathrm {c m} ^ {- 1} \\ \end{array}
$$

# C.3 POWER DENSITY

For a central core radius of 3 1/2 feet and a core height of 10 feet,

Total volume of the central core $= 1.09 \times 10^{7}$ cc

Assuming all 600 Mw of heat are generated in the central core,

Average power density in the core $= 55.2\mathrm{w / cc}$

# C.4 NEUTRON FLUX

The average neutron fluxes in the central core for the hot, clean critical condition were computed as follows,

average rate of fission $= 55.2$ w/cc x 3.1 x 10 $^{10}$ fissions/w-sec

$$
= 1. 7 1 \times 1 0 ^ {1 2} \text {f i s s i o n s / c c - s e c}
$$

average rate of fission also equals: $\sum_{\mathbf{f}} \phi_{11} + \sum_{\mathbf{f}} \phi_{21} + \sum_{\mathbf{f}} \phi_{31}$ From Figure 4.3,

$$
\phi_ {2 1} = 0. 4 8 \phi_ {1 1}
$$

$$
\varnothing_ {3 1} = 0. 1 3 \varnothing_ {1 1}
$$

The values of $\sum f$ for the hot, clean critical uranium concentration for a 3 l/2-foot radius central core, $0.0088 \, \text{gm/cc}$ , were computed to be,

$$
\sum_ {f l l} = 1. 2 0 \times 1 0 ^ {- 4} c m ^ {- 1}
$$

$$
\sum_ {f 2 1} = 7. 6 3 \times 1 0 ^ {- 4} c m ^ {- 1}
$$

$$
\sum r 3 1 = 6. 8 0 \times 1 0 ^ {- 3} c m ^ {- 1}
$$

Making the appropriate substitutions and solving these equations, one obtains for the hot, clean critical condition of a 3 1/2-foot radius central core,

average fast flux in the central core, 100 ev to 2 mev:

$$
\varnothing_ {1 1} = 1. 2 4 \times 1 0 ^ {1 5} \text {n e u t r o n s / c c - s e c}
$$

average intermediate flux in the central core, 0.5 ev to 100 ev:

$$
\varnothing_ {2 1} = 5. 9 6 \times 1 0 ^ {1 4} \text {n e u t r o n s / c c - s e c}
$$

average thermal flux in the central core, up to 0.5 ev:

$$
\phi_ {3 1} = 1. 6 2 \times 1 0 ^ {1 4} \text {n e u t r o n s / c c - s e c}
$$

the percent of the total fissions occurring in each energy group:

$$
\frac {\sum_ {f} \phi x 1 0 0}{\text {t o t a l f i s s i o n s / c c - s e c}} = \frac {\sum_ {f} \phi x 1 0 0}{1 . 7 1 x 1 0 ^ {1 2}}
$$

Fast group 9%

Intermediate group 27%

Thermal group 64%

# C.5 FUEL BURN-UP

Approximately 1.3 grams of uranium-235 are consumed to produce 1 Mw day of energy. At 600 Mw power, the uranium burn-up will be approximately 780 g/day. This is equivalent to burning up a quantity of uranium approximately equal to the total uranium inventory in the fuel system every year.

# c.6 EVALUATION OF FISSION PRODUCT POISONING

An extended analysis of the fission product nuclear poison build-up for conditions similar to those of this design study has been performed (Ref. 33 and 34). This analysis is for a reactor using uranium-233 as fuel. The fission product yield for U-235 is so nearly equal to that of U-233 that this factor was neglected. This analysis assumes complete removal of all gaseous fission products, which should very nearly be the condition for the reactor of this design study. The results of this reference are presented as the "poison fraction" build-up with time of operator for the reactor considered.

$$
\text {P o i s o n f r a c t i o n ,} \mathrm {P} = \frac {\sum_ {\mathrm {P}}}{\sum_ {\mathrm {F}}} = f (\mathrm {t})
$$

The fission product production, and therefore $\sum_{\mathbb{P}}$ , is directly proportional to the power density of the reactor fuel system, which in turn is proportional to

the product of neutron flux and fission cross section, $\Sigma_{\mathrm{f}}\phi$ . If it is assumed that the primary means of fission product removal is by neutron absorption and only a small portion is removed by radioactive decay, the quantity of fission product poison, $\Sigma_{\mathrm{p}}$ , present is approximately inversely proportional to neutron flux, $\phi$ .

The poison fraction build-up with time is then seen to be very nearly independent of operating conditions of different neutron flux, fuel concentration and power density,

$$
P = \frac {\sum P}{\sum f} = f (t) \frac {(\sum f \phi) (\frac {1}{\phi})}{\sum f} = f (t)
$$

The plot of poison fraction increase with time given in Reference 34, page 14, should, therefore, be applicable to this design study's reactor as a first approximation.

The data presented in Reference 34 extends only to 150 days of operation. A visual extrapolation of the curve for total fission product poisoning would indicate that the curve asymptotically approaches a poison fraction of about 15 percent for very long periods of operation ( $>5$ years). This is probably a conservative estimate. Any plating of the metallic fission products in the low flux, heat exchanger region (see Section 2.6.0) would help to reduce this value.

Assuming little or no corrosion, and therefore corrosion product nuclear poisoning to be small, the value of 15 percent poison fraction may be used to approximate the negative reactivity introduced in this design study's reactor after very long periods of operation ( $>5$ years).

Converting poison fraction to units of reactivity (Ref. 8, p. 262),

$$
z = \frac {\sum_ {u}}{\sum_ {m}} \cong 2 \text {(f o r t h i s d e s i g n s t u d y)}
$$

$$
\text {r e a c t i v i t y}, \rho \cong - \frac {\mathrm {P} z}{1 + z} \cong - \frac {0 . 1 5 x 2}{1 + 2} \cong - 0. 1 0
$$

From Figure 4.l of this report, it may be seen that it will require a uranium concentration of approximately 0.0115 g/cc to override this negative reactivity and maintain criticality after a very long period of operation.

This is equivalent to about 131 percent of the hot, clean critical inventory.

# C.7 REPROCESSING CYCLE TIME

The excess uranium fuel inventory that is added to override fission product and corrosion product nuclear poisons should be the economic optimum that considers the costs of fuel reprocessing. The following economic analysis is based on the fuel reprocessing method available today (Section 2.6.0) which requires contaminated salt solvent to be discarded and replaced.

Cost of replacement salt $\cong$ $5 per lb

Cost of recovering uranium $\cong$ $1 per lb

from contaminated salt by UF4volatility process (Ref. 38)

Total \(\equiv\) \\(6 per lb

Wt of salt in fuel system ≈ $1.85 x 10⁵ lb

Cost of replacing all of the solvent salt in the fuel system ≈ $1.1 x 106

Assuming an interest charge of 4 percent per year on the cost of uranium fuel inventory,

$$
\begin{array}{l} \text {O p t i m u m c o s t o f u r a n i u m i n v e n t o r y i n} \cong \\ \text {e x c e s s o f t h a t f o r t h e h o t , c l e a n} \\ \text {c r i t i c a l c o n d i t i o n , s o t h a t t h e a c c u m u l a t e d i n t e r e s t e n t h a n g l e o f t h e c o s t o f f u e l r e p r o c e s s - i n g (r e p l a c i n g s a l t)} \end{array}
$$

where, $Y =$ cycle time, in years, to replace all the fused salt solvent in the fuel system.

Assuming $20 per gram for highly enriched uranium-235, this is,

$$
\begin{array}{l l} \text {O p t i m u m u r a n i u m i n v e n t o r y i n e x c e s s o f} & \cong \quad 1. 4 \times 1 0 ^ {6} g \\ \text {t h a t f o r t h e h o t , c l e a n c o n d i t i o n} & \end{array}
$$

$$
\text {V o l u m e} \quad 2. 7 \times 1 0 ^ {7} \mathrm {c c}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l l} \text {O p t i m u m u r a n i u m c o n c e n t r a t i o n i n e x c e s s} & \cong \frac {0 . 0 5 1 g / c c}{Y} \\ \text {o f h o t , c l e a n c r i t i c a l c o n d i t i o n a f t e r} & \text {r e p r o c e s s i n g e q u i l i b r i u m i s a t t a i n e d} \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \text {U r a n i u m c o n c e n t r a t i o n f o r h o t , c l e a n} \\ \text {c r i t i c a l c o n d i t i o n} \end{array} \cong 0. 0 0 8 8 g / c c
$$

Equating the sum of these uranium concentrations to that required to override the fission product poison (Section C.6),

$$
\begin{array}{l} \text {T h e o p t i m u m c y c l e t i m e t o r e p l a c e a l l} \cong 1 9 \text {y e a r s} \\ \text {t h e s o l v e n t s a l t i n t h e f u e l s y s t e m} \end{array}
$$

It would initially require a period of time equal to the time of one reprocessing cycle, during which no fuel reprocessing is performed, to attain this balance of uranium inventory and fission product poisons.

# APPENDIX D

# PRIMARY HEAT EXCHANGER DESIGN

# D.1.O DERIVATION OF FORMULAE FOR PARAMETRIC STUDY

The following basic formulae were used considering sodium coolant on the tube side and fused salt fuel on the shell side.

D.1.1. Continuity Equation

$$
W = \rho A V \tag {D.1}
$$

D.1.2 Flow Area on Shell Side for Triangular Pitch

$$
A = \left[ 1 / 2 \dot {P} _ {\mathrm {T}} x 0. 8 6 7 P _ {\mathrm {T}} - 1 / 2 \pi \frac {d _ {0} ^ {2}}{4} \right] p e r 1 / 2 t u b e \tag {D.2}
$$

$$
A = \left[ . 8 6 7 P _ {T} ^ {2} - \frac {\pi}{4} d _ {o} ^ {2} \right] p e r t u b e.
$$

D.1.3 Equivalent Diameter for Triangular Pitch for Shell and Tube Sides

Shell Side:

$$
d _ {e} = 4 \frac {\text {f l o w a r e a}}{\text {w e t t e d p a r a m e t e r}} = \frac {4 \times 1 / 2 \left[ . 8 6 7 P _ {T} ^ {2} - \frac {\pi}{4} d _ {o} ^ {2} \right]}{1 / 2 d _ {o}} \tag {D.3}
$$

where, $P_{\mathrm{T}} = d_{0} + s$

$$
d _ {e} = 4 \underbrace {\left(8 6 7 P _ {T} ^ {2} - \frac {\pi}{4} d _ {o} ^ {2}\right)} _ {\pi d _ {o}}
$$

Tube Side:

$$
\mathrm {d} _ {\mathrm {e}} = \mathrm {d} _ {\mathrm {j}} \tag {D.4}
$$

D.l.4 Over-all Heat Transfer Coefficient for Duplex Tube Based on Outside Surface

$$
\begin{array}{l} \frac {1}{U _ {o}} = \frac {1}{h _ {s}} + \frac {r _ {o}}{k} \left(\ln \frac {r _ {o}}{r _ {i}}\right) _ {N i} + \left(\frac {r _ {o}}{k} \ln \frac {r _ {o}}{r _ {i}}\right) _ {S S} \frac {r _ {o} N i}{r _ {o} S S} + \frac {1}{h _ {N a} \left(\frac {r _ {i}}{r _ {o}}\right)} \tag {D.5} \\ \frac {1}{U _ {o}} = \frac {1}{h _ {s}} + \frac {r _ {o}}{k _ {N i}} \ln \frac {r _ {o}}{r _ {m}} + \frac {r _ {o}}{k _ {S S}} \ln \frac {r _ {m}}{r _ {i}} + \frac {r _ {o}}{h _ {N a} r _ {i}} \\ \end{array}
$$

D.1.5 Film Coefficient between Fluid and Surface

Shell side - Dittus-Boelter Equation

$$
\mathrm {N u} = \frac {\mathrm {h} \mathrm {d e}}{\mathrm {k}} = 0. 0 2 3 \left(\frac {\mathrm {d e V}}{\mu}\right) ^ {0. 8} \left(\frac {\mathrm {c _ {p}}}{\mathrm {k}}\right) ^ {0. 4} \tag {D.6}
$$

Tube side - Martinelli and Lyon Equation

$$
\mathrm {N u} = \frac {\mathrm {h d} _ {\mathrm {e}}}{\mathrm {k}} = 7 + 0. 0 2 5 \left(\frac {\rho \mathrm {d} _ {\mathrm {e}} V}{\mu}\right) ^ {0. 8} \left(\frac {\mathrm {c} _ {\mathrm {p}}}{\mathrm {k}}\right) ^ {0. 8} \tag {D.7}
$$

D.1.6 Heat Transfer Equation

$$
q = U _ {o} S _ {o} (\triangle t _ {o l}) \tag {D.8}
$$

D.1.7 Surface Area Required for Heat Transfer

$$
\mathrm {S} _ {\mathrm {O}} = \pi \mathrm {d} _ {\mathrm {O}} \mathrm {L N} \tag {D.9}
$$

D.1.8 Combining the Above Basic Equations into Working Formulae

Then from (D.1) and (D.2) with constants for the conditions of this design, the equations become:

D.1.9 Salt Flow

$$
w _ {S} = \rho_ {S} \left[ . 8 6 7 P _ {T} ^ {2} - \frac {\pi}{4} d _ {0} ^ {2} \right] v _ {S} x N \tag {D.10}
$$

# D.1.10 Sodium Flow

$$
w _ {N a} = e _ {N a} \frac {\pi}{4} d _ {1} ^ {2} v _ {N a} N \tag {D.11}
$$

# D.1.11 Salt Heat Transfer Coefficient

From (D.6),

$$
h _ {S} = 0. 0 2 3 \frac {\mathrm {k}}{\mathrm {d} _ {e}} \left(\frac {\rho \mathrm {d} _ {c} V _ {C}}{\mu}\right) ^ {0. 8} \left(\frac {c _ {p} \mu}{\mathrm {k}}\right) ^ {0. 4} \tag {D.12}
$$

$$
h _ {S} = 0. 0 2 3 \frac {k}{d _ {e}} \left(\frac {\rho d _ {e}}{\mu}\right) ^ {0. 8} \left(\frac {c _ {D} \lambda}{k}\right) ^ {0. 4} v _ {S} ^ {0. 8} a c _ {1} v _ {S} ^ {0. 8}
$$

then,

$$
h _ {S} = c _ {1} v _ {S} ^ {0. 8} \tag {D.13}
$$

$$
C _ {1} = 0. 0 2 3 \frac {\mathrm {k}}{\mathrm {d} _ {\mathrm {e}}} \left(\frac {\rho \mathrm {d} _ {\mathrm {e}}}{\mu}\right) ^ {0. 8} \left(\frac {\mathrm {c} _ {\mathrm {p}}}{\mathrm {k}}\right) ^ {0. 4} \tag {D.14}
$$

# D.1.12 Sodium Heat Transfer Coefficient from (D.7)

$$
h _ {N a} = 7 \frac {k}{d _ {e}} + 0. 0 2 5 \frac {k}{d _ {e}} \left(\frac {\rho d _ {e} V}{\mu}\right) ^ {0. 8} \left(\frac {c _ {p} \lambda}{k}\right) ^ {0. 8} \tag {D.15}
$$

$$
h _ {N a} = c _ {2} + c _ {3} v ^ {0. 8}
$$

then,

$$
C _ {2} = 7 \frac {k}{d _ {e}} \text {a n d} C _ {3} = 0. 0 2 5 \frac {k}{d _ {e}} \left(\frac {\rho d _ {e}}{\mu}\right) ^ {0. 8} \left(\frac {c _ {p} / k}{k}\right) ^ {0. 8} \tag {D.16}
$$

# D.1.13 Over-all Heat Transfer Coefficient in Terms of the Number of Tubes

From (D.8) and (D.9),

$$
q = U _ {0} \pi d _ {0} L N (\Delta t _ {0 l}) \tag {D.17}
$$

For a given $\mathbf{P}_{\mathrm{T}}$ and $\mathbf{d}_0$ , and for the flows required for heat transfer, from (D.10),

$$
V _ {S} = \frac {C _ {4}}{N} \tag {D.18}
$$

and

$$
V _ {N a} = \frac {C _ {5}}{N} \tag {D.19}
$$

$$
C _ {4} = \frac {w _ {S}}{e _ {S} \left[ 8 6 7 P _ {T} ^ {2} - \frac {\pi}{4} d _ {0} ^ {2} \right]} \tag {D.20}
$$

where,

$$
C _ {5} = \frac {w _ {\mathrm {N a}}}{\rho_ {\mathrm {S}} \frac {\pi}{4} d _ {1} ^ {2}} \tag {D.21}
$$

from (D.17),

$$
U _ {O} N L = C _ {6} \tag {D.22}
$$

where,

$$
C _ {6} = \frac {q}{\pi d _ {\circ} (\triangle t _ {\circ} l)} \tag {D.23}
$$

from (D.5), (D.14) and (D.15),

$$
\frac {1}{U _ {o}} = \frac {1}{c _ {1} v _ {S} ^ {0 . 8}} + R _ {N i} + R _ {S S} + \frac {1}{c _ {7} + c _ {8} v _ {N a} ^ {0 . 8}} \tag {D.24}
$$

$$
R _ {N i} = \frac {r _ {o}}{k _ {N i}} \ln \frac {r _ {o}}{r _ {m}} \tag {D.25}
$$

$$
R _ {S S} = \frac {r _ {0}}{k _ {S S}} \ln \frac {r _ {m}}{r _ {1}} \tag {D.26}
$$

193.

$$
\mathrm {C} _ {7} = \mathrm {C} _ {2} \frac {\mathrm {r} _ {\mathrm {i}}}{\mathrm {r} _ {\mathrm {o}}} \tag {D.27}
$$

$$
c _ {8} = c _ {3} \frac {r _ {1}}{r _ {0}} \tag {D.28}
$$

Rewriting (D.24), inserting (D.18) and (D.19),

$$
\frac {1}{U _ {0}} = \frac {1}{C _ {1} \left(\frac {C _ {4}}{N}\right) ^ {0 . 8}} + R _ {N i} + R _ {S S} \frac {1}{C _ {7} + C _ {8} \left(\frac {C _ {5}}{N}\right) ^ {0 . 8}} \tag {D.29}
$$

Therefore, $\mathbf{U}_0$ is a function of $\mathbf{N}$ since,

$$
\frac {1}{U _ {0}} = f (N), \text {t h e n a l s o} U _ {0} = f (N) \tag {D.30}
$$

But from (D.22) and (D.30), then,

$$
f (N) N \times L = C _ {6}
$$

Now, as L is evaluated at some arbitrary value to agree with the requirements of the problem,

$$
f (N) N = \frac {C _ {6}}{L} \tag {D.31}
$$

which is known, then there is only one value of $\mathbf{N}$ to satisfy the conditions of the two simultaneous equations (D.22) and (D.29) with $\mathbf{L}$ established. These two equations can be solved either by iteration or by graphical means. The latter procedure was selected for this study.

# D.1.14 Calculation of Pressure Drop and Horsepower Due to Friction

Sodium pressure drop,

$$
\Delta p = (2. 0 + f L) \frac {v _ {N a} ^ {2}}{2 g} \frac {\rho}{1 4 4} \tag {D.32}
$$

combining with (D.19),

$$
\Delta p = (2. 0 + c _ {1 0}) \frac {c _ {9}}{N ^ {2}} \tag {D.33}
$$

where,

$$
c _ {9} = c _ {5} ^ {2} \cdot \frac {\ell}{1 4 4 \times 2 g} \tag {D.34}
$$

$$
\mathrm {C} _ {1 0} = \mathrm {f} \frac {\mathrm {L}}{\mathrm {d} _ {\mathrm {e}}} \times 1 2 = 2 4 0 \frac {\mathrm {f}}{\mathrm {d} _ {\mathrm {e}}} \tag {D.35}
$$

where $\mathbf{L} = 20$ feet, $\mathbf{d}_2$ in inches.

Horespower,

$$
P h p = \frac {\Delta p \times 144}{C _ {N a}} \times \frac {w _ {N a}}{5 5 0} \tag {D.36}
$$

where,

$$
C _ {1 1} = \frac {1 4 4}{e _ {\mathrm {N a}}} x \frac {w _ {\mathrm {N a}}}{5 5 0} (1 b / \sec) \tag {D.37}
$$

$$
\Delta \mathrm {h p} = \mathrm {c} _ {1 1} \Delta \mathrm {p} \tag {D.38}
$$

Salt pressure drop, combining with (D.20) and (D.32)

$$
\Delta p = \left[ 2. 0 + c _ {1 3} \right] \frac {c _ {1 2}}{N _ {2}} \tag {D.39}
$$

where,

$$
c _ {1 2} = \frac {c _ {4} ^ {2} e}{1 4 4 x 2 g} \tag {D.40}
$$

$$
C _ {1 3} = f \frac {L}{d _ {e}} x 1 2 = 2 4 0 \frac {f}{d _ {e}} \tag {D.41}
$$

where $\mathbf{L} = 20$ feet.

Horsepower,

$$
P _ {h p} = c _ {1 4} \Delta p \tag {D.42}
$$

where,

$$
C _ {1 4} = \frac {1 4 4}{E S} x \frac {W S (1 b / s e c)}{5 5 0}. \tag {D.43}
$$

# D.2.0 PRIMARY HEAT EXCHANGER SAMPLE CALCULATIONS

Solution of the two simultaneous equations discussed in Section D.1.13 results in a heat exchanger with 14,600 5/8-inch O.D. duplex tubes with an 1/8-inch ligament and .065-inch wall as the optimum and most applicable to this design. However, actual layout of the heat exchanger tube bundle indicated that 24 exchangers with 650 tubes each was a practical arrangement. Nevertheless, the following sample calculation is based on a heat exchanger of 14,600 tubes with 0.065-inch duplex wall with 0.042-inch Type 304 stainless steel and 0.023-inch Type "L" nickel clad.

# D.2.1 Properties

Fuel Mixture Side

Salt No. 34, 47 mol % NaF and 53 mol % ZrF<sub>4</sub>

Average Properties

$$
\mathrm {C} = 1 9 5 \mathrm {l b / f t 3}, / ^ {\prime} = 1 9 \mathrm {l b / h r - f t}, \mathrm {C _ {p}} = 0. 2 8 5 \mathrm {B T U / l b - ^ {\circ} F},
$$

$$
k = 1. 3 0 \mathrm {B T U} / \mathrm {h r} - \mathrm {f t} ^ {2} - \mathrm {o F} / \mathrm {f t}
$$

Inlet Temperature = 1200°F Outlet Temperature = 1050°F

Sodium Coolant Side

Average properties

$$
e = 5 1. 0 \mathrm {l b} / \mathrm {f t} ^ {3}, \mu = 0. 5 3 \mathrm {l b} / \mathrm {h r} - \mathrm {f t}, \mathrm {C} _ {\mathrm {p}} = 0. 3 0 \mathrm {B T U} / \mathrm {l b} - \mathrm {o} _ {\mathrm {F}},
$$

$$
k = 3 6. 2 \mathrm {B T U / h r - f t ^ {2} - o F / f t}
$$

Inlet Temperature $= 1000^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ Outlet Temperature $= 1150^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$

Inlet Enthalpy = 338.5 BTU/lb Outlet Enthalpy = 293.7 BTU/lb

# D.2.2. Flows

Sodium

$$
w _ {N a} = \frac {q}{\Delta H}
$$

$$
w _ {N a} = \frac {6 0 0 , 0 0 0 \times 3 4 1 3}{3 3 8 . 5 - 2 9 3 . 7} = 4 5. 7 \times 1 0 ^ {6} l b / h r = 1 2. 7 \times 1 0 ^ {3} l b / s e c
$$

Salt

$$
w _ {S} = \frac {q}{c _ {p} (\Delta t)}
$$

$$
w _ {S} = \frac {6 0 0 , 0 0 0 x 3 4 1 3}{0 . 2 8 5 (1 2 0 0 - 1 0 5 0)} = 4 8. 0 x 1 0 ^ {6} l b / h r = 1 3. 3 x 1 0 ^ {3} l b / s e c
$$

# D.2.3. Velocities

Sodium

$$
V _ {N a} = \frac {w N a}{\left(\mathrm {A} _ {1} \mathrm {N}\right)} \quad A _ {1} = \frac {\pi}{4} d _ {1} ^ {2} \quad N = N o. o f t u b e s
$$

$$
\mathrm {V} _ {\mathrm {N a}} = \frac {4 5 . 7 \times 1 0 ^ {6} \times 4 \times 1 4 4}{3 6 0 0 \times 5 1 . 0 \times (. 6 2 5 - . 1 3) ^ {2} \times 1 4 , 6 0 0} = 1 2. 8 \mathrm {f t / s e c}
$$

Salt

$$
V _ {S} = \frac {w _ {S}}{c A _ {O} N} \quad A _ {O} = . 8 6 7 P T ^ {2} - \frac {\pi d _ {O} ^ {2}}{4}
$$

$$
v _ {S} = \frac {4 8 . 0 x 1 0 ^ {6} x 1 4 4}{3 6 0 0 x 1 9 5 x (. 8 6 7 x . 7 5 ^ {2} - \frac {\pi}{4} x . 6 2 5 ^ {2}) 1 4 , 6 0 0} = 3. 7 f t / s e c
$$

# D.2.4 Equivalent Diameter

Sodium

$$
d _ {e} = d _ {i} = . 6 2 5 - . 1 3 = 0. 4 9 5 \mathrm {i n}.
$$

Salt

$$
d _ {e} = \frac {4 (A _ {0})}{\text {p e r i m e t e r}} = \frac {4 (. 8 6 7 P _ {T} ^ {2} - \pi / 4 d _ {o} ^ {2})}{\pi d _ {o}}
$$

$$
d _ {e} = \frac {4 (. 8 6 7 x . 7 5 ^ {2} - \pi / 4 x . 6 2 5 ^ {2})}{\pi x . 6 2 5} = 0. 3 6 8 \ln .
$$

# D.2.5 Reynolds Number

Sodium

$$
R e = \frac {\rho d _ {e} v _ {N a}}{\mu}
$$

$$
\mathrm {R e} = \frac {5 1 . 0 \times 0 . 4 9 5 \times 1 2 . 8 \times 3 6 0 0}{1 2 \times . 5 3} = 1 8 3, 0 0 0
$$

Salt

$$
\mathrm {R e} = \frac {\rho d _ {e} V _ {S}}{\mu}
$$

$$
\mathrm {R e} = \frac {1 9 5 \times . 3 6 8 \times 3 . 7 \times 3 6 0 0}{1 2 \times 1 9} = 4 2 0 0
$$

# D.2.6 Heat Transfer Coefficients and Wall Resistance

Sodium

$$
\frac {h _ {\mathrm {N a}} d _ {\mathrm {e}}}{k} = 7 + 0. 0 2 5 (\mathrm {R e}) \cdot 8 (\mathrm {P r}) \cdot 8
$$

$$
\text {R e} x \Pr = P e
$$

$$
\Pr = \frac {C _ {p} / \mu}{k}
$$

$$
h _ {N a} = \frac {3 6 . 2 x 1 2}{. 4 9 5} \left[ 7 + 0. 0 2 5 (1 8 3, 0 0 0) \cdot^ {8} \left(\frac {. 3 0 x . 5 3}{3 6 . 2}\right) ^ {. 8} \right] = 1 0, 7 5 0
$$

Salt

$$
\frac {\mathrm {h} _ {\mathrm {s}} \mathrm {d} _ {\mathrm {e}}}{\mathrm {k}} = 0. 0 2 3 (\mathrm {R e}) ^ {. 8} (\mathrm {P r}) ^ {. 4}
$$

$$
h = \frac {1 . 3 x 1 2}{. 3 6 8} \left[ 0. 0 2 3 (4 2 0 0) ^ {\cdot 8} \left(\frac {. 2 8 5 x 1 9}{1 3}\right) ^ {\cdot 4} \right] = 1 3 6 0
$$

Wall resistance based on external tube surface

$$
k _ {S S} = 1 2. 9 B T U / h r - f t ^ {2} - F / f t
$$

$$
k _ {N i} = 3 3. 5 B T U / h r - f t ^ {2} - F / f t
$$

$$
R _ {N i} = \frac {r _ {O}}{k _ {N i}} \ln \frac {r _ {O}}{r _ {m}} = \frac {. 3 1 0}{3 3 . 5 \times 1 2} \quad \ln \frac {. 3 1 2}{. 2 8 9} = 0. 5 9 5 \times 1 0 ^ {- 4}
$$

$$
R _ {N i} = \frac {1}{1 6 , 7 0 0}
$$

$$
R _ {S S} = \frac {r _ {o}}{k _ {S S}} \ln \frac {r _ {m}}{r _ {i}} = \frac {. 3 1 0}{1 2 . 0 x 1 2} \ln \frac {. 2 8 9}{. 2 4 7} = 3. 1 4 x 1 0 ^ {- 4}
$$

$$
R _ {S S} = \frac {1}{3 1 8 0}
$$

D.2.7 Over-all Heat Transfer Coefficient Based on External Tube Surfac

$$
\frac {1}{U _ {o}} = \frac {1}{h S} + R _ {N 1} + R _ {S S} + \frac {1}{h _ {N a} \frac {r _ {1}}{r _ {o}}} = \frac {1}{1 3 6 0} +. 5 9 5 \times 1 0 ^ {- 4} + 3. 1 4 \times 1 0 ^ {- 4} + \frac {1}{1 0 , 7 5 0} \left(\frac {. 2 4 7}{. 3 1 2}\right)
$$

$$
\frac {1}{U _ {0}} = 7. 3 5 \times 1 0 ^ {- 4} +. 5 9 5 \times 1 0 ^ {- 4} + 3. 1 4 \times 1 0 ^ {- 4} + 1. 1 8 \times 1 0 ^ {- 4}
$$

$$
U _ {o} = 8 1 5 B T U / h r - f t ^ {2} - F
$$

D.2.8 Effective Length of Tube Required for Heat Transfer

$$
\mathbf {q} = \mathbf {u} _ {\circ} \mathbf {s} _ {\circ} (\Delta \mathbf {t} _ {\circ \ell})
$$

$$
\mathrm {S} _ {\mathrm {O}} = \pi \mathrm {d} _ {\mathrm {O}} \mathrm {N L}
$$

$$
L = \frac {q}{U _ {0} \pi d _ {0} N (\Delta t _ {o l})} = \frac {6 0 0 , 0 0 0 x 3 4 1 3 x 1 2 .}{8 1 5 x \pi x . 6 2 5 x 1 4 , 6 0 0 x 5 0}
$$

$$
L = 2 1 f t
$$

# D.2.9 Pressure Drop Through Tube Bundle

Sodium

$$
\Delta p = (2. 0 + f L) \frac {v _ {N a} ^ {2}}{2 g} \cdot \frac {\rho}{1 4 4}
$$

with $\text{Re} = 183,000$ , $\mathbf{f} = 0.016$ (Ref. 16, p. 15).

Salt

$$
\begin{array}{l} \Delta p = (2. 0 + f \frac {L}{d _ {e}}) \frac {V _ {S} ^ {2}}{2 g} \frac {e}{1 4 4} \\ \Delta p = (2. 0 + . 0 4 0 x \frac {2 1 x 1 2}{. 3 6 8}) \frac {3 . 7 ^ {2}}{2 g} x \frac {1 9 5}{1 4 4} = 8. 5 p s i \\ \end{array}
$$

# D.2.10 Pumping Power Required for Heat Exchanger Only

Sodium

$$
P h p = \frac {\Delta p x 1 4 4 w _ {N a}}{e 5 5 0} = \frac {9 . 1 x 1 4 4 x 1 2 . 7 x 1 0 ^ {3}}{5 1 x 5 5 0} = 5 9 4 h p
$$

Fuel

$$
P _ {h p} = \frac {\Delta p x 1 4 4 w s}{e 5 5 0} = \frac {8 . 5 x 1 4 4 x 1 3 . 3 x 1 0 ^ {3}}{1 9 5 x 5 5 0} = 1 5 2 h p
$$

# D.2.11 Fuel Holdup in Tube Bundle

$$
\text {S a l t a r e a f l o w} A _ {o S} = \left[ . 8 6 7 P _ {T} ^ {2} - \frac {\pi}{4} d _ {o} ^ {2} \right] N
$$

Salt volume Vol $\pm$ Aos x L, assuming L = 21.0 ft

$$
\begin{array}{l} \operatorname {V o l} = \left[ . 8 6 7 x. 7 5 ^ {2} - \frac {\pi}{4}. 6 2 5 ^ {2} \right] \cdot \frac {1 4 , 6 0 0 x 2 1}{1 4 4} \\ V o l = 3 8 5 f t ^ {3} \\ \end{array}
$$

# D.2.12 Sizing the Fuel Circulating Pumps

<table><tr><td>Pressure drop in tube bundle</td><td>8.5 psi</td></tr><tr><td>Outer flow periphery</td><td>1.0</td></tr><tr><td>Check valve</td><td>2.8</td></tr><tr><td>Contingency</td><td>2.7</td></tr><tr><td>Total</td><td>15.0 psi</td></tr></table>

# Total pump power

Assume pump efficiency at $60\%$

$$
P h p = \frac {\Delta p x 1 4 4 w _ {S}}{c 5 5 0 \eta} \quad w _ {S} = 1 3. 3 \times 1 0 ^ {6} l b / s e c
$$

$$
P h p = \frac {1 5 x 1 4 4 x 1 3 . 3 x 1 0 ^ {3}}{1 9 5 x 5 5 0 x . 6 0} = 4 4 6 h p
$$

# With 8 pumps

Horsepower each pump - 56 hp

Specify 60 hp motor driven pumps

# APPENDIX E

# CALCULATIONS FOR PRELIMINARY DESIGN OF INTERMEDIATE HEAT EXCHANGER

# E.1 DESIGN PARAMETERS

The intermediate heat exchangers are designed to transfer $600\mathrm{Mw}$ of heat from radioactive sodium in the primary loop to non-radioactive sodium in the intermediate loop. The design if based upon the following, previously selected sodium temperatures and heat exchange rate:

Primary Na entering $\mathbf{t_{h1}}$ 1150F

Primary Na leaving $t_{h2}$ 1000oF

Intermediate Na entering $t_{\dot{c}_1}$ 6750F

Intermediate Na leaving $t_{c_2}$ 1100F

Total heat to be transferred --- 2.048 x $10^{9}$ BTU/hr

Heat losses from the reactor vessel, primary loop piping and immediate heat exchanger have been ignored.

The basic assumption was made to use six intermediate heat exchangers.

# E.2 PROPERTIES OF SODIUM AND 304 SS

# Sodium

Temperature, ${}^{\circ}\mathbf{F}$ 1150 1100 1000 675

Density, 1b/ft3 49.87 50.30 41.15 53.92

Enthalpy, BTU/1b 338.5 323.6 293.7 195.7

Specific Heat, BTU/1b 0.2983 0.2983 0.2988 0.3052

Thermal Conductivity, 35.7 36.4 37.7 42.1

Viscosity, lb/ft-sec x 103 0.136 0.141 0.152 0.201

# 304 Stainless Steel

Thermal Conductivity 13.2 13.0 12.6 11.3 BTU/hr-ft²-F/ft

# E.3 SODIUM FLOWS

a. Primary loop flow $= \frac{q}{\Delta H} = \frac{2.048 \times 10^{9} \mathrm{BTU/hr}}{(338.5 - 293.7) \mathrm{BTU/lb}} = 45.7 \times 10^{6} \mathrm{lb/hr}$   
b. Intermediate loop flow $\frac{q}{\Delta H} = \frac{2.048 \times 10^{9} \mathrm{BTU/hr}}{(323.6 - 195.7) \mathrm{BTU/hr}} = 16.0 \times 10^{6} \mathrm{lb/hr}$

# E.4 SODIUM VELOCITIES IN HEAT EXCHANGER

Preliminary calculations indicated that 1100 1/2-inch O.D. tubes with 0.042-inch wall appear to be satisfactory.

a. Primary sodium velocity (shell side)

Assume that the tubes are in a triangular pattern with a 3/4-inch pitch

Using Figure B.4 for 1100 tubes,

$$
\frac {\text {R a d i u s o f t u b e b u n d l e}}{\text {T u b e P i t c h}} = \frac {\mathrm {R}}{\mathrm {P}} = 1 7. 4
$$

$$
R = (1 7. 4) (P) = (1 7. 4) (3 / 4) = 1 3. 0 5 \text {i n 。}
$$

Shell I.D. = tube bundle dia + tube dia + 2 in. free space

$$
\begin{array}{l} = (2) (1 3. 0 5) + 0. 5 0 + 2. 0 \\ = 2 8. 6 \text {i n .} (\text {U s e} 3 0 \text {i n .}) \\ \end{array}
$$

Shell cross section area $= (0.785)(\frac{30}{12})^2 = 4.91 ft^2$

Total tube cross section area = $\frac{(0.785)(0.5)^2(1100)}{144} = 1.50 ft^2$

Shell side flow area $= 4.91 - 1.50 = 3.41 ft^2$

Na flow per exchanger $= \frac{45.7 \times 10^{6}}{6} = 7.62 \times 10^{6} \mathrm{lb/hr}$

Average Na density $= \frac{49.87 + 51.15}{2} = 50.51$ lb/ft³

Shell side velocity = $\frac{(7.62 \times 10^{6} \cdot 1b / hr)}{(50.51 \cdot 1b / ft^{3})(3.41 \cdot ft^{2})(3600 \cdot sec / hr)}$

$$
= 1 2. 2 9 f t / s e c
$$

b. Intermediate sodium velocity (tube side)

Flow area per tube $= 0.1358\ln^{2}$

Tube side flow area $= \frac{(0.1358)(1100)}{144} = 1.037\mathrm{ft}^2$

Na flow per exchanger $= \frac{16.0 \times 10^{6} \mathrm{lb/hr}}{6} = 2.67 \times 10^{6} \mathrm{lb/hr}$

Average Na density = 50.30 + 53.92 = 52.11 lb/ft³

Tube side velocity $= \frac{(2.67 \times 10^{6} \, \text{lb/hr})}{(52.1 \, \text{lb/ft}^{3})(1.037 \, \text{ft}^{2})(3600 \, \text{sec/hr})}$   
= 13.73 ft/sec

# E.5 EQUIVALENT DIAMETER FOR SHELL SIDE

$$
d _ {e} = \frac {4 x \text {s h e l l s i d e f l o w a r e a}}{\text {w e t t e d p e r i m e t e r}} = \frac {(4) (3 . 4 1 f t ^ {2}) (1 4 4 \text {i n .} ^ {2} / f t)}{(1 1 0 0) (3 . 1 4) (0 . 5 \text {i n .})}, = 1. 1 3 7 \text {i n}.
$$

# E.6 REYNOLDS NUMBER

Primary Sodium (shell side)

Average Na viscosity = $\left(\frac{0.136 + 0.152}{2}\right) \times 10^{-3} = 0.144 \times 10^{-3} \, \text{lb/ft-sec}$

$$
\mathrm {R e} = \frac {\mu_ {\mathrm {d}} \rho}{\bar {\mu}} = \frac {(1 2 . 2 9 \mathrm {f t / s e c}) (1 . 1 3 7 \mathrm {i n .}) (5 0 . 5 1 \mathrm {l b / f t ^ {3}})}{(1 2 \mathrm {i n / f t}) (0 . 0 0 0 1 4 4 \mathrm {l b / f t - s e c})} = 3. 7 5 \times 1 0 ^ {5}
$$

Intermediate Sodium (tube side)

Average Na viscosity = $\left(\frac{0.141 + 0.201}{2}\right) \times 10^{-3} = 0.171 \times 10^{-3} \, \text{lb/ft-sec}$

$$
R e = \frac {A d \rho}{\bar {\mu}} = \frac {(1 3 . 7 3 f t / s e c) (0 . 4 1 6 i n .) (5 2 . 1 1 l b / f t ^ {3})}{(1 2 i n / f t) (0 . 0 0 0 1 7 1 l b / f t - s e c)} = 1. 4 5 x 1 0 ^ {5}
$$

# E.7 HEAT TRANSFER COEFFICIENTS

Film Coefficient Primary Sodium (shell side)

Average specific heat = $\left(\frac{0.2983 + 0.2988}{2}\right) = 0.2985$ BTU/lb-F

Average thermal conductivity $= \left(\frac{35.7 + 37.7}{2}\right) = 36.7$ BTU/hr-ft²-F/ft

204.

$$
\Pr = \frac {\overline {{C}} _ {p} \mu}{\overline {{K}}} = \frac {(0 . 2 9 8 5 \mathrm {B T U / l b - F}) (0 . 0 0 0 1 4 4 \mathrm {l b / f t - s e c}) (3 6 0 0 \mathrm {s e c})}{(3 6 . 7 \mathrm {B T U / h r - f t ^ {2} - F / f t}) (\mathrm {h r})} = 0. 0 0 4 2 5
$$

Using an empirical correlation for shell side of unbaffled liquid metal

heat exchangers (Ref. 15, p. 285),

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mathrm {N u} = 0. 2 1 2 \left[ \left(\mathrm {d} _ {\mathrm {e}}\right) (\mathrm {R e}) (\mathrm {P r}) \right] ^ {0. 6} = 0. 2 1 2 \left[ (1. 1 3 7) (3. 7 5 \times 1 0 ^ {5}) (0. 0 0 4 2 5) \right] ^ {0. 6} \\ \mathrm {N u} = \frac {\mathrm {h} _ {\mathrm {D}}}{\mathrm {k}} = 1 9. 0 8 \\ h _ {0} = 1 9. 0 8 \left(\frac {\overline {{k}}}{d _ {e}}\right) = \frac {(1 9 . 0 8) (3 6 . 7 B T U / h r - f t - F) (1 2 i n / f t)}{(1 . 1 3 7 i n .)} = 8 4 0 2 B T U / h r - f t ^ {2} - F \\ \end{array}
$$

Film Coefficient Intermediate Sodium (tube side)

Average specific heat = 0.2983 + 0.3052 = 0.3018 BTU/1b-F

Average thermal conductivity = $\frac{36.4 + 42.1}{2} = 39.3$ BTU/hr-ft-F

Using Lyon-Martinelli relation for liquid metals and a uniform wall

heat flux,

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mathrm {P e} = \frac {\mathrm {d} \kappa \overline {{c}} \overline {{c}} _ {\mathrm {p}}}{\overline {{\mathbf {k}}}} = \frac {(0 . 4 1 6 \text {i n .}) (1 3 . 7 3 f t / s e c) (5 2 . 1 1 l b / f t ^ {3}) (0 . 3 0 1 8 B T U / l b - F) x 3 6 0 0 \frac {\mathrm {s e c}}{\mathrm {h r}}}{{(1 2 i n / f t)} (3 9 . 3 B T U / h r - f t - F)} \\ \mathrm {P e} = 6 8 5. 7 \\ N u = 7 + 0. 0 2 5 P e ^ {0. 8} \\ N u = \frac {h _ {D}}{k} = 7 + (0. 0 2 5) (6 8 5. 7) ^ {0. 8} = 1 1. 6 2 \\ h _ {1} = 1 1. 6 2 \frac {(k)}{(d)} = \frac {(1 1 . 6 2) (3 9 . 3 B T U / h r - f t - F) (1 2 i n / f t)}{(0 . 4 1 6 i n .)} \\ h _ {1} = 1 3, 1 7 3 B T U / h r - f t ^ {2} - F \\ \end{array}
$$

Tube Wall Thermal Conductivity

$$
\begin{array}{l} \text {A v e r a g e t h e r m a l c o n d u c t i v i t y} = \frac {1 3 . 2 + 1 3 . 2 + 1 2 . 6 + 1 1 . 3}{4} \\ = 1 2. 5 2 \mathrm {B T U} / \mathrm {h r} - \mathrm {f t} - \mathrm {F} \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \frac {1}{U _ {o}} = \frac {d _ {o}}{h _ {1} d _ {1}} + \frac {d _ {o} \ln \frac {d _ {o}}{d _ {1}}}{K _ {\text {t u b e}}} + \frac {1}{h _ {o}} \\ = \frac {(0 . 5 0)}{(1 3 , 1 7 3) (0 . 4 1 6)} + \frac {(0 . 5 0) \ln \frac {0 . 5 0}{0 . 4 1 6}}{(1 2 . 5 2) (1 2)} + \frac {1}{8 4 0 2} \\ = 0. 0 0 0 0 9 1 2 + 0. 0 0 0 6 1 2 + 0. 0 0 0 1 1 9 \\ = 0. 0 0 0 8 2 2 2 \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
U _ {o} = 1 2 1 6 B T U / h r - f t ^ {2} - F
$$

# E.9 . HEAT TRANSFER SURFACE AREA

Logarithmic Mean Temperature Difference

$$
\begin{array}{l} \Delta t = \frac {\left(t _ {h _ {2}} - t _ {c _ {1}}\right) - \left(t _ {h _ {1}} - t _ {c _ {2}}\right)}{\ln \left(\frac {t _ {h _ {2}} - t _ {c _ {1}}}{t _ {h _ {1}} - t _ {c _ {2}}}\right)} \\ = \frac {(1 0 0 0 - 6 7 5) - (1 1 5 0 - 1 1 0 0)}{\ln \left(\frac {1 0 0 0 - 6 7 5}{1 1 5 0 - 1 1 0 0}\right)} \\ = 1 4 7 ^ {\circ} \mathrm {F} \\ \end{array}
$$

Total Heat Transfer Surface

$$
q = U _ {O} S _ {O} \Delta t
$$

$$
s _ {t} = \frac {q}{U _ {0} \Delta t} = \frac {(2 . 0 4 8 \times 1 0 ^ {9} \cdot B T U / h r)}{(1 2 1 6 B T U / h r - f t ^ {2} - F) (1 4 7 ^ {\circ} F)} = 1 1, 4 5 7 f t ^ {2}
$$

Heat Transfer Surface per Heat Exchanger

$$
s = \frac {1 1 . 4 5 7}{6} = 1 9 1 0 f t ^ {2}
$$

E.10 EFFECTIVE TUBE LENGTH

$$
L = \frac {S}{(N) (T u b e p e r i m e t e r)} = \frac {(1 9 1 0 f t ^ {2}) (1 2 i n / f t)}{(1 1 0 0 t u b e s) (0 . 5 0 i n .) (3 . 1 4)} = 1 3. 2 7 f t
$$

# E-11 SODIUM PRESSURE DROP THROUGH HEAT EXCHANGER

Pressure Drop for Primary Sodium (shell side)

From Section D.6, the Reynolds number for the shell side is $3.75 \times 10^{5}$ and the corresponding friction factor is $f = .014$ .

From Reference 39, Chapter 1.5, the pressure drop is given by,

$$
\begin{array}{l} \Delta P = \frac {f L}{d _ {e}} x \frac {\rho v ^ {2}}{2 g} \\ \Delta P = \left(\frac {0 1 4 x 1 3 . 2 7}{1 . 1 3 7}\right) x \left(\frac {5 0 . 5 1 x 1 2 . 2 9 ^ {2}}{6 4 . 4 x 1 2}\right) \\ \triangle P = 1. 6 1 \mathrm {p s i} \\ \end{array}
$$

Pressure Drop for Intermediate Sodium (tube side)

From Section D.6, the Reynolds number for the tube side is $1.45 \times 10^{5}$ and the corresponding friction factor is $f = .017$ . In this case, the pressure drop is given by Equation (5.8),

$$
\Delta P = \left(\frac {2 + f L}{d _ {e}}\right) \times \frac {\rho v ^ {2}}{2 g}
$$

where 2 is the number of velocity leads allowed for the tube sheet entrance and exit losses and the $180^{\circ}$ bend.

$$
\begin{array}{l} \Delta P = \left(\frac {2 + . 0 1 7 \times 1 3 . 2 7 \times 1 2}{. 4 1 6}\right) \times \left(\frac {5 2 . 1 1 \times 1 3 . 7 3 ^ {2}}{6 4 . 4 \times 1 4 4}\right) \\ \triangle P = 9. 0 2 \mathrm {p s i} \\ \end{array}
$$

# APPENDIX F

# STEAM GENERATOR CALCULATIONS

# F.1 NATURAL CIRCULATION BOILER WITH SEPARATE SUPERHEATER

From superheater heat balance,

$$
8 _ {\text {b o i l e r s}} = 1 6, 0 0 0, 0 0 0 (1 2 7. 9 - 4 2) = 1, 3 7 2, 0 0 0, 0 0 0 \mathrm {B T U} / \mathrm {h r}
$$

Assume two boilers generating saturated steam at 2,000 psia, steam on the shell side and sodium on the tube side. Say design is for $1000^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ and 2000 psi external pressure, the thickness of a tube for external pressure can be calculated (Ref. 35, paragraph UG-31).

For 304 SS,

"S" allowable = 8800 psi (Ref. 35, Table UHA-23)

$\therefore \underline{\mathbf{X}} \cong .19$ (min)

∴For 3/4 in. O.D. tubes, X min = .143

Use .165 average wall (8 BWG)

For a Single Wall Separating the Fluids Wall Resistance,

$$
\frac {r _ {0}}{k} \ln \frac {r _ {0}}{r _ {1}} = \frac {0 . 3 7 5}{1 2 . 4 x 1 2} \quad \ln \cdot 7 5 0 = . 0 0 1 4 6
$$

# Water Side

For present purposes, the assumption will be made that the unit operates in the nucleate boiling region and later on this assumption will be verified. In order to determine a reasonable value of the average boiling and scale coefficients for design, resort will be made to both the PWR and HRT designs.

# HRT

From ORNL-LR-Dwg-2795A, there are 251 3/8-inch 0.D. x .065-in. wall type 347 SS tubes of 20 ft effective length.

Surface (actual outside) = $\frac{\pi x \cdot 375}{12} x 251 \times 20 = 494$ sq ft

On Page (8) of Spec. HRT-1004, the fluid properties, flow rates and temperatures are given,

![](images/1748ade64806b59f19f2b6993a65ae623a6c7d2954592d16e2e202bff6cfcbbb.jpg)

$$
\Delta t _ {o l} = \frac {7 7 . 5}{\ln \frac {1 0 1}{2 3 5}} = 5 3. 2
$$

$$
U (\text {D e s i g n}) = \frac {1 . 7 1 \times 1 0 ^ {7}}{4 9 4 \times 5 3 . 2} = 6 5 2
$$

Tube Wall

$$
\frac {r _ {0}}{k} \ln \frac {r _ {0}}{r _ {1}} = \frac {. 1 8 7 5}{1 0 . 6 \times 1 2} \ln \frac {. 3 7 5}{. 2 4 5} = . 0 0 6 2 5
$$

Primary Fluid

$$
\frac {h d}{k} = . 0 2 3 \left(\frac {c _ {p} \mu}{k}\right) ^ {. 4} \left(\frac {d V \rho}{\mu}\right) ^ {. 8}
$$

Flow area = $\frac{\pi}{4} (.245)^{2}$ x $\frac{251}{144} = .0822$ sq ft

Velocity = 1.79 x 10^5 / 3.6 x 10^3 x .0822 x 53.2 = 11.4 fps

h = .339 x .023 x 12 $\left(\frac{1.23 \times .266}{.339}\right)^4$ $\left(\frac{\frac{245}{12} \times 53.4 \times 11.4 \times 3600}{.266}\right)^8 = 5800$

h (based on outside surface) = 5800 x .245 = 3790

$$
\frac {1}{3 7 9 0} = . 0 0 0 2 6 4
$$

The over-all U for the HRT was based on using $3 / 4 \times U$ clean.

$$
\begin{array}{l} \therefore \frac {1}{6 5 2 \times 4 / 3} = . 0 0 1 1 5 = \frac {1}{U \text {c l e a n}} \\ (w a l l \quad a n d \quad p r i m a r y \quad r e s i s t a n c e) = . 0 0 8 9 0 \\ \therefore \frac {1}{U \text {c l e a n}} = . 0 0 0 8 9 0 = . 0 0 0 2 6 \\ h (\text {B o i l i n g}) = \frac {1}{. 0 0 0 2 6 x 3 / 4} = 5 1 0 0; \text {s a y , b o i l i n g c o e f f i c i e n t} \\ \end{array}
$$

A similar calculation on the PWR design (Ref. 36) indicates that these units had a design boiling plus scale coefficient of the order of magnitude of 2000. This indicates a scale coefficient of the order of magnitude of 3300 average was used on the steam side of these units. For the present design, an average value of scale plus nucleate boiling coefficient of 2000 is used. This should be conservative for design purposes, since the higher average temperature differentials in this unit should yield higher boiling coefficients than either the PWR or HRT designs.

For a natural circulation boiler, the steam water mixture will be external to the tubes and the sodium will be internal to the tubes. On the basis of preliminary calculations, it appeared that a reasonable size and pressure drop would be realized in two units with approximately 2000 3/4-inch O.D. tubes to carry in each.

Sodium Side

$$
\begin{array}{l} \frac {h d}{k} = 7 +. 0 2 5 \left(\frac {d V}{f ^ {2}} c _ {p}\right) ^ {. 8} \\ d = \frac {0 . 4 2}{1 2} f t \\ K = 4 0 B T U / h r - f t ^ {2} - _ {O F} ^ {-} / f t \\ C _ {P} = . 3 0 2 (\text {a v g}) B T U / 1 b ^ {- o} F \\ V = 2 1. 9 \text {f p s} \\ \mu = . 1 7 8 \times 3 6 0 0 \times 1 0 ^ {- 3} = . 6 4 \mathrm {l b s / f t - h r} \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
\rho_ {\text {a v g}} = 5 2. 7 \mathrm {l b s} / \mathrm {f t} ^ {3}
$$

$$
h = \frac {1 2}{4 2} x 4 0 \left[ 7 +. 0 2 5 \left(\frac {4 2}{1 2} x 2 1. 9 x 5 2. 7 x \frac {3 6 0 0}{4 0} x . 3 0 2\right) ^ {. 8} \right]
$$

$$
\mathrm {h} = 1 5, 7 0 0
$$

h based on outside tube surface $= 15,700 \times \frac{42}{.75} = 8800$

$$
\frac {1}{8 8 0 0} = . 0 0 0 1 1 3
$$

Flow area = $\frac{\pi x (.42)^2}{4} x \frac{2000}{144} = 1.93$ sq ft

$$
V = \frac {8 , 0 0 0 , 0 0 0}{3 6 0 0 \times 1 . 9 3 \times 5 2 . 7} = 2 1. 9 f p s
$$

$$
\frac {1}{h} = (\text {B o i l i n g} + \text {s c a l e}) = . 0 0 0 5
$$

$$
\frac {1}{U _ {0}} = 0. 0 0 2 0 7 3
$$

$$
U _ {o} = 4 8 3
$$

Say, $\mathbf{U}_{\mathrm{o}}$ for design $= 1480$

Check of assumption of nucleate boiling,

At inlet $\triangle t = 324.2^{\circ}F$

$$
(q / 5) _ {\text {i n l e t}} = 3 2 4. 2 \times 4 8 0 = 1 5 6, 0 0 0
$$

As indicated (Ref. 4 and 6), this heat flux is still in the nucleate boiling region. As a further check, assume the tubes to be clean.

$$
\frac {1}{4 8 0} = . 0 0 2 0 8
$$

$$
\frac {1}{3 3 0 0} = . 0 0 0 3 0 3
$$

$$
(1 / U _ {0}) \text {c l e a n} = . 0 0 1 7 7 7
$$

$$
\therefore U _ {O} \text {c l e a n} = 5 6 4
$$

$$
\mathrm {q} / \mathrm {S} \text {i n l e t c l e a n} = 3 2 4. 2 \times 5 6 4 = 1 8 2, 5 0 0
$$

$$
\triangle t f i l m = \frac {1 8 2 , 5 0 0}{5 0 0 0} = 3 6. 6 ^ {\circ} F
$$

This is less than the critical $\triangle t$ of 45 to $50\mathrm{F}$ (Ref. 6, p. 386). Therefore, the assumption of nucleate boiling is justified.

Size of steam generator: $R / P = 20.9$ (Fig. B.4)

Diameter of channel: 20.9 x 2 x 1 + 2.75 = 44.6, say, 47 in. at

ID of channel

A preliminary check of the circulation characteristics of the steam generator indicated that a reasonable number of risers and downcomers can be provided to give adequate circulation for these units. The steam drums would each be approximately 66 inches ID x 45 feet over-all length to give commercial steam purities with commercial type feed water treatment. The center line of drums would be approximately 30 feet above the center line of the exchangers. Drum supporting steel will be provided and the support saddles on the heat exchangers will be designed so that one saddle is fixed to the foundation and one is let free to move to accommodate the expansion of the unit and/or the piping.

Over-all Heat Balance: Boiler and Superheater

# Sodium Side

The free area/tube = .866 x .75² - π/4 (1/2)² = .290

The perimeter/tube $= \frac{\pi}{2} = 1.57$

The equivalent dia $= \frac{4 \times \text{Area}}{\text{perimeter}} = \frac{4 \times .290}{1.57} = 0.74$ in.

For the sodium side heat transfer, the Liquid Metals Handbook indicates that the Lyon-Martinelli or some modification is satisfactory. Since the sodium is not the controlling resistance, and for the sake of consistency, the Lyon-Martinelli correlation will be used.

$$
\begin{array}{l} \frac {\mathrm {h d} _ {\mathrm {e}}}{\mathrm {R}} = 7 +. 0 2 5 \left(\frac {\mathrm {d} _ {\mathrm {e}} \mathrm {V P}}{\mathrm {R}} \mathrm {C} _ {\mathrm {p}}\right) ^ {. 8} \\ D _ {e} = \frac {0 . 7 4}{1 2} f t \\ P _ {a v g} \cong \frac {5 0 . 3 + 5 1 . 6 7}{2} = 5 0. 9 l b s / c u f t \\ R \cong \frac {3 6 . 4 + 3 8 . 2}{2} = 3 7. 3 B T U / h r - f t ^ {2} - o _ {F} / f t \\ C _ {p} = . 2 9 9 B T U / 1 b ^ {- o F} \\ \end{array}
$$

Based on main bank geometry,

Free area (total) = 1275 x 0.29 = 370 sq in.

$$
\mathbf {w} = \ell \mathbf {x} \mathbf {A} \mathbf {x} \mathbf {v}
$$

$$

$$

Resistance $= \frac{1}{8200} = .000122$

# Pressure Drop

The following calculations are for the pressure drop in the exchanger.

The pressure drop in the inlet and outlet nozzles is not included as part

of the pressure drop of the components. This is estimated elsewhere.

# Sodium Side

Reynolds No. = DVE = 0.74 x 17 x 3600 x 50.9 = 356,000

f = .014 for smooth tubes

$$
\begin{array}{l} \Delta p = \left(1. 5 + . 0 1 4 x \frac {2 1 . 1 x 1 2}{. 7 4}\right) \frac {v ^ {2}}{2 g} \frac {\rho}{1 4 4} = 6. 3 \frac {v ^ {2}}{2 g} \frac {\rho}{1 4 4} \\ = 6. 3 \times \frac {(1 7) ^ {2}}{6 4 . 4} \times \frac {5 0 . 9}{1 4 4} = 1 0 p s i \\ \end{array}
$$

For bend use 1/2 velocity head,

$\Delta P$ Bed $= \frac{0.79\text{psi}}{\text{say},\text{llpsi}}$ for exchanger only

# Steam Side

Assume flow and friction pressure drop are essentially that for an incompressible fluid. If the calculations on this assumption show a high pressure drop, then the variation of specific volume with tube length may have to be considered. The acceleration loss due to the change in specific volume between inlet and outlet will be considered.

In terms of our nomenclature and the assumed flow conditions, the general pressure drop equation becomes,

$$
P _ {1} - P _ {2} = f \frac {L}{d} \frac {V _ {\text {a v g}}}{2 g} ^ {2} \frac {\rho_ {\text {a v g}}}{1 4 4} + \left(\frac {W}{A}\right) ^ {2} \frac {1}{(3 6 0 0) 2 g} \frac {(V _ {2} - V _ {1})}{1 4 4} + \text {s a y}, 1. 5 \text {v e l h e a d s ,}
$$

entrance and exit + .5 vel heads bend

$$
c _ {a v g} = \frac {5 . 3 + 2 . 3}{2} = 3. 8
$$

$$
v _ {1} \cong . 1 8 8
$$

$$
v _ {2} \cong . 4 4 2
$$

$$
W = 1, 9 5 0, 0 0 0 \text {l b s / h r t o t a l}
$$

$$
A = \frac {1 0 7 5}{1 4 4} \times 1 2 7 5 = . 9 5 4 \mathrm {s q f t / u n i t}
$$

$$
V _ {\text {a v g}} = \frac {1 , 9 5 0 , 0 0 0}{3 6 0 0 x . 9 5 4 x 3 . 8} = 7 4. 8 \text {f p s}
$$

$$
\text {R e y m o l d s N o .} = \frac {\mathrm {D V C}}{\mathrm {u}} = \frac {0 . 3 7}{1 2} \times 7 4. 8 \times 3 6 0 0 \times 3. 8 = 4 8, 0 0 0
$$

$$
f = . 0 2 1 \text {f o r}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \Delta p = . 0 2 1 \times \frac {2 2 . 3 \times 1 2}{. 3 7} \times \frac {(7 4 . 8) ^ {2}}{2 g} \times \frac {3 . 8}{1 4 4} + \frac {2 (7 4 . 8) ^ {2}}{2 g} \times \frac {3 . 8}{1 4 4} \\ x \left(\frac {\frac {1 , 9 5 0 , 0 0 0}{2}}{3 6 0 0 x . 9 5 4}\right) ^ {2} x \left(\frac {. 4 4 2 - . 8 8}{1 4 4}\right) x \frac {1}{3 2 . 2} \\ = 1 7. 2 \frac {(7 4 . 8)}{2 g} ^ {2} x \frac {3 . 8}{1 4 4} + \frac {(2 8 4)}{1 4 4} ^ {2} x (0. 4 4 2 - 0. 1 8 8) x \frac {1}{3 2 . 2} \\ = 3 9. 4 + 4. 4 = 4 3. 8, \text {s a y}, 4 4 p s i \\ \end{array}
$$

Since this represents but a small part of the pressure of the system, it will be satisfactory to take the pressure drop as being this value. A more accurate calculation, taking into account the variation of specific volume with length, will give a higher pressure drop, but not significantly so. For system design purposes, assume a nominal 50 psi pressure drop on the steam side, not including nozzles.

# B. Two Walls Separating Both Fluids

The assumption made in this calculation will be the same as for the single wall separating the two fluids. Because of the thicker tube assembly, the $3/4$ -inch pitch cannot be used. It will be assumed that a $1/4$ -inch clear

ligament will be required for welding of these tubes into tube sheets. It will further be assumed that a thermal bond can be realized between the inner and outer tubes, but that this bond is not a mechanical bond. Thus, the failure of one wall will not give rise to a complete failure of the unit. It is further realized that radial temperature gradients, in some instances, may tend to cause separation of the two tube walls. However, in the superheater, it may be possible to use two different types of stainless steels with

![](images/8f0ba9f6bfcc613476dd48782e59da32eb907e1d272dbadff6546046eb0d226b.jpg)

coefficients of expansion in such a ratio as to negate the temperature differentials.

$$
\text {P i t c h} = . 6 6 6 +. 2 5 0 = . 9 1 6 \text {i n}.
$$

$$
\text {F o r} \quad \text {t u b e} \quad \text {s h e t} \quad \text {l a y o u t} \quad \text {a s s u m e} \quad \frac {1 5}{1 6} \text {i n . p i t c h (}. 9 3 7 5)
$$

Log Mean Temperature Difference = 191°F

For Preliminary Purposes, neglect sodium coefficient

Assume 1500 tubes to carry

$$

$$
\text {S t e a m f l o w} = 1. 9 5 5 \times 1 0 ^ {6} \mathrm {l b / h r}
$$

2. Sodium Flow

Na Temp. in = 1100°F   
Na Temp. out $= 675^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$

-30⁴ BTU x 4250F
1b-OF

Sodium flow = 15.85 x 10 $^6$ $\frac{1b}{hr}$

3. Change in Sodium Temperature through Preheat, Evaporator

and Superheater Zones

The change in sodium temperature in a zone is found from a heat balance between heat gained by the water and heat given up by the sodium.

(Na flow) C $\Delta t =$ (steam flow) $\triangle H$

$$
\Delta t _ {\mathrm {N a}} = \frac {W _ {\mathrm {s}} \Delta_ {\mathrm {H}}}{W _ {\mathrm {N a}} C _ {\mathrm {p}}}
$$

The sodium temperature change calculation is tabulated below:

<table><tr><td>Zone</td><td>H</td><td>Cp</td><td>t</td></tr><tr><td>preheat</td><td>236</td><td>.3090</td><td>95</td></tr><tr><td>evaporator</td><td>473</td><td>.3050</td><td>190</td></tr><tr><td>superheater</td><td>339</td><td>.3015</td><td>140/425°F</td></tr></table>

4. Calculation of Over-all Coefficient of Heat Transfer (U) in Preheat, Evaporator and Superheat Zones (Refer to Section 6.5.0)

A. Preheat Section

a. Since the water entering the boiler is subcooled, nuclease boiling will occur. A combined coefficient for boiling film and scale of 2000 BTU/hr-ft $^{2}$ - ${}^{0}$ F was used (Section 6.5.1).

b. Tube wall coefficient

$$
\begin{array}{l} k = 1 1. 2 B T U / h r - f t ^ {2} - ^ {\circ} F / f t \text {f o r} 3 0 4 S S \text {a t} 7 2 3 ^ {\circ} F \\ h = \frac {k}{f t} = \frac {1 1 . 2}{\frac {. 2 5}{1 2} \ln \frac {. 2 5}{. 1 8 5}} = 1 7 9 0 B T U / h r - f t ^ {2} - ^ {\circ} F \\ \end{array}
$$

c. Sodium film coefficient

$$
k = 4 1. 4 B T U / h r - f t ^ {2} \cdot {} ^ {0} F / f t
$$

$$
\mathrm {h} = \frac {\mathrm {k}}{\mathrm {d}} \left[ 7 + \left(\frac {0 . 0 2 5 \mathrm {D V} (\mathrm {C} _ {\mathrm {p}})}{\mathrm {k}}\right) ^ {0. 8} \right] \quad \text {(R e f . 7)}
$$

$$
d = 0. 0 6 1 8 f t
$$

$$
V = 1 3. 2 5 f t / s e c
$$

$$
\ell = 5 3. 6 \mathrm {l b} / \mathrm {f t} ^ {3}
$$

$$
C _ {p} = 0. 3 0 8 5 B T U / 1 b - ^ {\circ} F
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} h = \frac {4 1 . 4}{. 0 6 1 8} \left[ 7 + 0. 0 2 5 (\frac {. 0 6 1 8 \times 1 3 . 2 5 \times 5 3 . 6 \times 3 0 8 5}{4 1 . 4}) ^ {0. 8} \right] \\ = 5 4 0 0 \mathrm {B T U} / \mathrm {h r} - \mathrm {f t} ^ {2} - \mathrm {O F} \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \frac {1}{U} = \frac {1}{2 0 0 0 x . 1 8 5} + \frac {1}{5 4 0 0} + \frac {1}{1 7 9 0} = . 0 0 1 4 5 3 \\ U = 6 9 0 B T U / h r - f t ^ {2} - \alpha F \\ \end{array}
$$

# B. Boiling Section

a. Use a boiling film and scale coefficient of 2000 BTU/hr-ft²-OF   
b. Tube wall coefficient

$$
\begin{array}{l} k = 1 1. 8 B T U / h r - f t ^ {2} - o _ {F} / f t \text {f o r} 3 0 4 S S \text {a t} 8 6 5 ^ {\circ} F \\ h = \frac {k}{f t} = \frac {1 1 . 8}{\frac {. 2 5}{1 2} \ln \frac {. 2 5}{. 1 8 5}} = 1 8 9 0 B T U / h r - f t ^ {2} - ^ {\circ} F \\ \end{array}
$$

c. Sodium film coefficient

$$
\begin{array}{l} k = 3 9. 3 B T U / h r - f t ^ {2} - o _ {F} / f t \\ V = 1 3. 2 5 f t / s e c \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
d = . 0 6 1 8 f t
$$

$$
e = 5 2. 3 5 \mathrm {l b} / \mathrm {f t} ^ {3}
$$

$$
C _ {p} = 0. 3 0 4 7 B T U / 1 b - ^ {\circ} F
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mathrm {h} = \frac {\mathrm {k}}{\mathrm {d}} \cdot \left[ 7 + 0. 0 2 5 \left(\frac {\mathrm {D V} \ell \mathrm {C} _ {\mathrm {p}}}{\mathrm {k}}\right) ^ {0. 8} \right] \\ = \frac {3 9 . 3}{. 0 6 1 8} \left[ 7 + 0. 0 2 5 (\frac {. 0 6 1 8 \times 1 3 . 2 5 \times 5 2 . 3 5 \times . 3 0 4 7}{3 9 . 3}) ^ {0. 8} \right] \\ = 4 4 6 0 \mathrm {B T U} / \mathrm {h r} - \mathrm {f t} ^ {2} - \circ F \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \frac {1}{U} = \frac {1}{2 0 0 0 \times . 1 8 5} + \frac {1}{1 8 9 0} + \frac {1}{4 4 6 0} = 0. 0 0 1 4 2 9 \\ U = 7 0 0 B T U / h r - f t ^ {2} - o F \\ \end{array}
$$

# C. Superheater Section

a. Steam film coefficient

$$
\frac {\mathrm {h d} _ {\mathrm {e}}}{\mathrm {k}} = 0. 0 2 3 \left(\frac {\mathrm {d} _ {\mathrm {e}} \mathrm {v}}{\mu}\right) ^ {0. 8} \left(\frac {\mathrm {c} _ {\mathrm {p}} \mu}{\mathrm {k}}\right) ^ {1 / 3}
$$

Steam temp. out = 1000°F

Steam temp. in = 620°C

Average temp. = 810°F

$$
\mathrm {h d} ^ {0. 2} = \mathrm {Y} (\mathrm {W} / 1 0 0 0 \mathrm {a}) ^ {0. 8}
$$

$$
W = \frac {2 \times 1 0 ^ {6}}{3 2 0 0} = 6 2 5 \mathrm {l b / h r p e r t u b e}
$$

$$
Y = 1 6 5
$$

$$
d = d i a, \text {i n .} = 0. 5 - 2 \times 0. 0 6 5 = 0. 3 7 \text {i n .}
$$

A = flow area in.² = 0.1076 in.²

$$
\begin{array}{l} h x (. 3 7) ^ {0. 2} = 1 6 5 (6 2 5 / . 0 7 1 6) ^ {0. 8} \\ h = 1 1 0 0 B T U / h r - f t ^ {2} - o F \\ \end{array}
$$

b. Tube wall coefficient

$$
h = \frac {k}{f t} = \frac {1 1 . 8}{\frac {. 2 5}{1 2} \ln \frac {. 2 5}{. 1 8 5}} = 1 8 9 0 B T U / h r - f t ^ {2} - ^ {\circ} F
$$

c. Sodium film coefficient

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mathrm {h} = \frac {\mathrm {k}}{\mathrm {d}} \left[ 7 + 0. 0 2 5 \left(\frac {\mathrm {d v} \ell c _ {\mathrm {p}}}{\mathrm {k}}\right) ^ {0. 8} \right] \\ d = 0. 0 6 1 8 f t \\ V = 1 3. 5 6 f t / s e c \\ \ell = 5 1. 0 \mathrm {l b} / \mathrm {f t} ^ {3} \\ k = 3 7. 3 B T U / h r - f t ^ {2} - o _ {F} / f t \\ C _ {p} = 0. 3 0 1 5 B T U / 1 b ^ {\circ} F \\ h = \frac {3 7 . 3}{. 0 6 1 8} \left[ 7 + 0. 0 2 5 (\frac {. 0 6 1 8 \times 1 3 . 5 6 \times 5 1 . 0 \times . 3 0 1 5 \times 3 6 0 0}{3 7 . 3}) ^ {0. 8} \right] \\ = 8 4 5 0 \mathrm {B T U} / \mathrm {h r} - \mathrm {f t} ^ {2} - \mathrm {C F} \\ \frac {1}{U} = \frac {1}{1 1 0 0 x \cdot \frac {1 8 5}{2 5}} + \frac {. 2 5 \ln \frac {. 2 5}{. 1 8 5}}{3 7 . 3} + \frac {1}{8 4 5 0} = . 0 0 3 3 5 8 \\ U = 3 0 0 B T U / h r - f t ^ {2} - a F \\ \end{array}
$$

5. Calculation of Zone Surface Required

In a zone, the heat gained by water equals the heat lost by sodium and this heat is transferred by the equation, $q = UA \Delta t$ , where $\Delta t$ is the log mean temperature difference.

<table><tr><td>q</td><td>U</td><td>t</td><td>A</td><td>%A</td></tr><tr><td>4608 x 10^5</td><td>690</td><td>178.5</td><td>3,741 ft^2</td><td>17.7</td></tr><tr><td>9252 x 10^5</td><td>700</td><td>221.0</td><td>5,981 ft^2</td><td>28.2</td></tr><tr><td>6624 x 10^5</td><td>300</td><td>192.5</td><td>11,470 ft^2</td><td>54.1</td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td></td><td>21,192 ft^2</td><td>100.0%</td></tr></table>

6. Number of Tubes

As a result of checking pressure drop and steam velocities, a tube size of 1/2-inch OD and nominal 50-foot length was decided upon.

Total tube area required from (5) above $= 21,192 ft^2$

Area per tube, $\pi \mathrm{DL} = 6.54\mathrm{ft}^2$

No. of tubes $= \frac{21192}{6.54} = 3240$ tubes

7. Pressure Drop in Tubes (Ref. 7, p. 36, 45, 55, 69) (Section 6.5.2)

The fraction of the 50-foot tube length required by the preheat, evaporator and superheat zones is proportional to the percent of area of (5) above.

a. Preheat zone

$$
\begin{array}{l} R e = \frac {2 6 , 8 0 0}{\mu} = \frac {2 6 , 8 0 0}{. 2 3 9} = 1 1 2, 0 0 0 \\ f = 0. 1 9 \\ \Delta p = \frac {F V ^ {2}}{2 g} \frac {L}{d _ {e}} \rho \\ \Delta p = \frac {. 0 1 9 x . 5 . 1 4 ^ {2} x 8 . 8 5 x 4 7 . 1}{6 4 . 4 x . 0 3 0 8} \\ \Delta p = 1 0 5 \mathrm {l b} / \mathrm {f t} ^ {2} = . 7 3 \mathrm {p s i} \\ \end{array}
$$

b. Evaporator zone (two phase flow)

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mathrm {R e} = \frac {2 6 , 8 0 0}{\mu} = \frac {2 6 , 8 0 0}{. 2 0 2} = 1 3 2, 5 0 0 \\ f = . 0 1 8 \\ \Delta p _ {0} = \frac {. 0 1 8 x 6 . 1 3 ^ {2} x 1 4 . 1 x 3 9 . 5}{6 4 . 4 x . 0 3 0 8} \\ \Delta p _ {0} = 1 9 0. 4 \mathrm {l b} / \mathrm {f t} ^ {2} (\text {s i n g l e p h a s e}) \\ \Delta P _ {T P} = \Delta P _ {O} \left(\frac {\Delta P _ {t} p _ {f}}{\Delta P _ {O}}\right) + r \left(\frac {G}{g}\right) ^ {2} \quad (R e f. 7, p. 7 2) \\ \Delta p _ {T P} = 1 9 0. 4 (6) + . 2 \times \frac {(2 4 2) ^ {2}}{3 2 . 2} \\ \Delta p _ {T P} = 1 5 0 6 \mathrm {l b} / \mathrm {f t} ^ {2} = 1 0. 5 \mathrm {p s i} (\text {t w o p h a s e}) \\ \end{array}
$$

c. Superheater zone (Ref. 7, p. 69)

$$
R e = \frac {2 6 , 8 0 0}{\mu} = \frac {2 6 , 8 0 0}{. 0 6 5} = 4 1 3, 0 0 0
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} f = . 0 1 5 \\ \Delta p = \frac {G ^ {2}}{2 g d _ {e}} f L v + \frac {G ^ {2}}{g} (v _ {2} - v _ {1}) \\ \Delta p = \frac {(2 4 2) ^ {2}}{6 4 . 4 x . 0 3 0 8} x. 0 1 5 x 2 7. 0 5 x. 3 0 5 ^ {\prime} \frac {(2 4 2) ^ {2}}{3 2 . 2} (.. 4 1 6 5 - .. 1 9 5 0) \\ \Delta p = 4 0 5 9 \mathrm {l b} / \mathrm {f t} ^ {2} = 2 8. 2 \mathrm {p s i} \\ \end{array}
$$

d. Total tube pressure drop

The total drop is the sum of a, b, and c above.

(a) 0.73   
(b) 10.50   
(c) 28.20

Total Drop = 39.43 psi

# APPENDIX G

# CALCULATIONS OF STEAM PLANT HEAT BALANCE

The calculations below determine the heat balance and gross electric power output of the steam cycle described in Section 6.8.0 of this report. The over-all cycle and the conditions of the working fluids are shown in Figure 6.2.

# G.1 THROTTLE STEAM FLOW

The steam flow at the turbine throttle is calculated from Equation 6.11.

$$
\begin{array}{l} \text {S t e a m f l o w} = \frac {6 0 0 \mathrm {M w} \times 3 ^ {4} + 3 \times 1 0 ^ {3} \mathrm {B T U / M w - h r}}{(1 4 7 7 . 7 - 4 3 0 . 1) \mathrm {B T U / l b}} \\ \text {S t e a m f l o w} = 1. 9 5 5 \times 1 0 ^ {6} \frac {1 b}{h r} \\ \end{array}
$$

# G.2 EXTRACTION STEAM FLOWS

No. 1 Heater heat balance

$$
\begin{array}{l} 1. 9 5 5 \times 1 0 ^ {6} \frac {\mathrm {l b}}{\mathrm {h r}} (4 3 0. 1 - 3 6 4. 2) \frac {\mathrm {B T U}}{\mathrm {l b}} = W _ {1} (1 3 4 4 - 4 3 0. 1) \frac {\mathrm {B T U}}{\mathrm {l b}} \\ w _ {1} = . 1 4 1 x 1 0 ^ {6} \frac {1 b}{h r} \\ \end{array}
$$

No. 2 Heater heat balance

$$
\begin{array}{l} 1. 9 5 5 \times 1 0 ^ {6} (3 6 4. 2 - 3 0 0. 7) = . 1 4 1 \times 1 0 ^ {6} (4 3 0. 1 - 3 6 4. 2) \\ + w _ {2} (1 2 8 6 - 3 6 4. 2) \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
w _ {2} = . 1 2 4 6 \times 1 0 ^ {6} \mathrm {l b / h r}
$$

No. 3 Heater mass balance and heat balance

$$
\begin{array}{l} I. 1. 9 5 5 \times 1 0 ^ {6} = (. 1 4 1 + . 1 2 4 6) \times 1 0 ^ {6} + W _ {3} + F. W. \\ I I. 1. 9 5 5 \times 1 0 ^ {6} \times 3 0 0. 7 = (. 1 4 1 + . 1 2 4 6) \times 1 0 ^ {6} \times 3 6 4. 2 + 1 2 2 2 w _ {3} \\ + 2 3 8. 8 \mathrm {F}. \mathrm {w}. \\ \end{array}
$$

The solution of Equations I and II above gives,

$$
w _ {3} = . 0 8 9 \times 1 0 ^ {6} \frac {l b}{h r}
$$

F.W. = 1.6 x 10^6 lb (feed water flow leaving No. 3 Heater)

No. 4 Heater heat balance

$$
1. 6 \times 1 0 ^ {6} (2 3 8. 8 - 1 7 8. 0) = (1 1 6 4 - 2 4 3. 9) w _ {4}
$$

$$
w _ {4} = . 1 0 5 7 \times 1 0 ^ {6} \frac {1 b}{h r}
$$

No. 5 Heater heat balance

$$
1. 6 \times 1 0 ^ {6} (1 7 8. 0 - 1 1 7. 9) = . 1 0 5 7 \times 1 0 ^ {6} (2 4 3. 9 - 1 8 3. 1) + (1 0 9 5 \cdot 1 8 3. 1) W _ {5}
$$

$$
w _ {5} = . 0 9 8 4 \times 1 0 ^ {6} \frac {1 b}{h r}
$$

No. 6 Heater heat balance

$$
\begin{array}{l} 1. 6 \times 1 0 ^ {6} (1 1 7. 9 - 5 9. 7) = (. 1 0 5 7 + . 0 9 8 4) \times 1 0 ^ {6} (1 8 3. 1 - 1 2 2. 9) \\ + (1 0 2 4 - 1 2 2. 9) w _ {6} \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
W _ {6} = . 0 8 7 7 \frac {1 b}{h r}
$$

G.3 HEAT CONVERTED TO WORK IN TURBINE (Equation 6.10)

<table><tr><td>Position</td><td>Steam Flow (1b/hr)</td><td>△H (BTU/lb)</td><td>Work (BTU/hr)</td></tr><tr><td>Throttle</td><td>1.9550 x 106</td><td>136.3</td><td>266.5 x 106</td></tr><tr><td>No. 1 Heater</td><td>1.8140 x 106</td><td>58.0</td><td>105.2 x 106</td></tr><tr><td>No. 2 Heater</td><td>1.6894 x 106</td><td>64.0</td><td>108.1 x 106</td></tr><tr><td>No. 3 Heater</td><td>1.6004 x 106</td><td>58.0</td><td>92.8 x 106</td></tr><tr><td>No. 4 Heater</td><td>1.4947 x 106</td><td>69.0</td><td>103.1 x 106</td></tr><tr><td>No. 5 Heater</td><td>1.3963 x 106</td><td>71.0</td><td>99.1 x 106</td></tr><tr><td>No. 6 Heater</td><td>1.3066 x 106</td><td>82.6</td><td>107.1 x 106</td></tr></table>

Total of heat converted to work $= 881.9 \times 10^{6}$ BTU/hr

# G.4 CORRECTION TO WORK FOR FEED WATER HEATING BY PUMPS

a. Correction for Hot Well Pump

The work by the pump done on the feed water was found from Equation 6.14. $\mathbf{P}_{\mathbf{l}}$ is the pump inlet pressure of 1.5 inches Hg or .736 psi. $\mathbf{P}_{2}$ is the pump discharge pressure and is the sum of the three items listed below:

No. 3 heater pressure 103.1 psia

Elevation difference (cond. to No. 3 Heater) 17.3

Friction drop 40.0

$$
P _ {2} = 1 6 0. 4 \text {p s i a}
$$

The specific volume, $V_{1}$ , is .016l ft³.

$$
\text {W o r k} = (1 6 0. 4 - . 7 3 6) \times \frac {1 4 4}{7 7 8} x. 0 1 6 1
$$

$$
\text {W o r k} = . 4 7 6 \mathrm {B T U / 1 b}
$$

The work put into the pump was found from Equation 6.15.

$$
\text {W o r k} = \frac {. 4 7 6}{. 7} = . 6 7 6 \mathrm {B T U / l b}
$$

The heat gain of the feed water from the pump was found to be .2 BTU/lb by Equation 6.12.

The reduction in steam extracted for No. 6 Heater was found from Equation 6.16.

$$
W = \frac {1 . 6 x 1 0 ^ {6} x . 2}{(1 0 2 4 - 1 2 2 . 9)}
$$

$$
w = . 0 0 0 3 5 5 x 1 0 ^ {6} \frac {1 b}{h r}
$$

The increase in work at the turbine wheel was found from Equation 6.10.

$$
\text {W o r k} = . 0 0 0 3 5 5 \times 1 0 ^ {6} (1 0 2 4 - 9 4 1. 4)
$$

$$
\text {W o r k} = . 0 2 9 3 \times 1 0 ^ {6} \frac {\mathrm {B T U}}{\mathrm {h r}}
$$

# b. Correction for Boiler Feed Pump

This correction was made in the same manner as for the hot well pump above.

$$
\text {W o r k i n t o f e e d w a t e r} = (2 0 0 0 - 1 0 3. 1) \times \frac {1 4 4}{7 7 8} x. 0 1 7 6 6
$$

$$
\text {W o r k i n t o f e a d w a t e r} = 6. 2 0 \mathrm {B T U / l b}
$$

$$
\text {W o r k i n t o p u m p} = 8. 8 6 \mathrm {B T U / l b}
$$

$$
\text {H e a t} = 2. 6 6 \mathrm {B T U} / \mathrm {l b}
$$

The reduction in extracted steam is,

$$
W = \frac {1 . 9 5 5 x 1 0 ^ {6} x 2 . 6 6}{(1 2 8 6 - 3 6 4 . 2)}
$$

$$
w = . 0 0 5 6 4 x 1 0 ^ {6} \mathrm {l b / h r}
$$

The increase in turbine work is,

$$
\text {W o r k} = . 0 0 5 6 4 \times 1 0 ^ {6} x (1 2 8 6 - 9 4 1. 4)
$$

$$
\text {W o r k} = 1. 9 \times 1 0 ^ {6} \frac {\mathrm {B T U}}{\mathrm {h r}}
$$

The turbine work calculated in Section G.3 was corrected as shown below.

$$
\text {U n c o r r e c t e d} \quad \text {t u r b i n e w o r k (S e c t i o n G . 3)} \quad 8 8 1. 9 \times 1 0 ^ {6} \mathrm {D T U / h r}
$$

$$
\text {H o t w e l l p u m p c o r r e c t i o n} \quad + \quad . 0 3
$$

$$
\text {B o i l e r f e e d p u m p c o r r e c t i o n} \quad + 1. 9
$$

$$
\text {C o r r e c t e d} = 8 8 3. 8 3 \times 1 0 ^ {6} \mathrm {B T U / h r}
$$

This is equivalent to an output of 258.96 Mw.

# G.5 CORRECTION FOR TURBINE-GENERATOR LOSSES

The losses found by the method of Reference 41 were applied to the corrected turbine wheel output of Section G.4 as shown below.

Corrected output at turbine wheel 258.96 Mw

Turbine exhaust loss 8.35 Mw

Mechanical and generator losses 5.19

Total losses

Gross Electric Power Output 245.42 Mw

# G.6 CALCULATION OF COOLING WATER PUMP POWER

The cooling water pump power was found from Equations 6.14 and 6.15. A pressure drop of 10 feet was assumed in the condenser (Ref. 23). The cooling water flow was calculated from a heat balance between the heat rejected to the condenser by the turbine exhaust and No. 6 Heater drain and the heat gained by the cooling water. In calculating the required cooling water flow, a $15^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ rise in temperature was assumed. This could be greater, or less, depending upon the temperature and quantity of cooling water available and the type of condenser selected, i.e., single or double pass.

a. Heat rejected to condenser by turbine exhaust

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mathbf {q} = \mathbf {W} \triangle \mathbf {H} \\ q = 1. 3 1 2 6 \times 1 0 ^ {6} \mathrm {l b / h r} (9 4 1. 4 - 5 9. 7) \mathrm {B T U / l b} \\ q = 1 1 5 7 \times 1 0 ^ {6} B T U / h r \\ \end{array}
$$

b. Heat rejected to condenser by No. 6 Heater drain

$$
\begin{array}{l} q = . 2 9 1 4 \times 1 0 ^ {6} \frac {1 b}{h r} (1 2 2. 9 - 5 9. 7) B T U / l b \\ q = 1 8. 4 \times 1 0 ^ {6} B T U / h r \\ \end{array}
$$

c. Total heat rejected to condenser. The total rejected heat is the sum of Items (a) and (b) above, or 1175.4 x 10⁶ BTU/hr.

d. Cooling water flow to condenser

$$
\begin{array}{l} q = W C _ {p} \Delta t \\ w = \frac {1 1 7 5 . 4 x 1 0 ^ {6}}{1 x 1 5} = 7 8. 4 x 1 0 ^ {6} \frac {1 b}{h r} \\ \end{array}
$$

e. Work done by cooling water pump

$$
\begin{array}{l} \text {W o r k} = \left(P _ {1} - P _ {2}\right) \mathrm {V W} \\ \text {W o r k} = 1 0 \mathrm {f t} \times 6 2. 4 \frac {\mathrm {l b}}{\mathrm {f t} ^ {3}} \times . 0 1 6 1 \frac {\mathrm {f t} ^ {3}}{\mathrm {l b}} \times \frac {\mathrm {B T U}}{7 7 8 \mathrm {f t} / \mathrm {l b}} \times 7 8. 4 \times 1 0 ^ {6} \frac {\mathrm {l b}}{\mathrm {h r}} \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
\text {W o r k} = 1. 0 0 2 x. 1 0 ^ {6} \frac {\mathrm {B T U}}{\mathrm {h r}} = . 2 9 4 \mathrm {M w}
$$

f. Work put into cooling water pump. From Equation 6.15,

$$
\text {W o r k i n} = \frac {. 2 9 4 \mathrm {M w}}{. 6} = . 4 9 0 \mathrm {M w}
$$

G.7 POWER REQUIRED BY PUMP MOTORS (steam cycle)

The motor power was found by dividing the required pump power by the motor efficiency.

a. Cooling water pump motor

$$
\text {p o w e r} = \frac {. 4 9 0 M w}{. 9 5} = . 5 1 5 M w
$$

b. Hot well (condensate) pump motor

$$
\text {p o w e r} = \frac {. 6 7 6 \frac {\mathrm {B T U}}{1 b} x 1 . 6 x 1 0 ^ {6} \frac {1 b}{h r} x \frac {M w - h r}{3 . 4 2 3} x 1 0 ^ {6} \mathrm {B T U}}{. 9 5}
$$

$$
\text {p o w e r} = . 3 3 4 \mathrm {M w}
$$

c. Boiler feed water pump motor

$$
\text {p o w e r} = \frac {8 . 8 6 \frac {\mathrm {B T U}}{1 \mathrm {b}} \times 1 . 9 5 5 \times 1 0 ^ {6} \frac {1 \mathrm {b}}{\mathrm {h r}} \times \frac {\mathrm {M w - h r}}{3 . 4 1 3} \times 1 0 ^ {6} \mathrm {B T U}}{. 9 5}
$$

$$
\text {p o w e r} = 5. 3 4 \mathrm {M w}
$$

The total motor power required by the above pumps in the steam cycle is 6.19 Mw.

# SODIUM Piping PRESSURE DROP CALCULATIONS

Sample calculations: (Ref. 7)

# H.1 PIPE LINE PRESSURE LOSS

Primary loop: 8 in Schedule 40, 304 SS, 15 ft long

$$
T = 1 1 5 0 ^ {\circ} F
$$

$$
\rho = 4 9. 9 \mathrm {l b} / \mathrm {f t} ^ {3}
$$

$$
\mu = . 0 0 0 1 3 \mathrm {l b} / \mathrm {f t} - \sec
$$

$$
E / D = . 0 0 0 0 6
$$

$$
D = 0. 6 6 5 f t
$$

$$
V = 3 0. 5 f t / s e c
$$

$$
R e = \frac {D V C}{\mu} = \frac {. 6 6 5 \times 3 0 . 5 \times 4 9 . 9}{. 0 0 0 1 3} = 7. 7 8 \times 1 0 ^ {6}
$$

$$
f = . 0 1 1
$$

$$
\Delta P = f \frac {L}{D} \frac {v ^ {2}}{2 g} \frac {\rho}{1 4 4} p s i = . 0 1 1 x \frac {1 5}{. 6 6 5} x \frac {(3 0 . 5) ^ {2}}{6 4 . 4} x \frac {4 9 . 9}{1 4 4} = 1. 2 5 p s i
$$

# H.2 EXPANSION LOSS

Hot leg to heat exchanger. Assume one velocity heat loss for expansion.

$$
\Delta P = K \frac {V _ {1} ^ {2}}{2 g} x \frac {p}{1 4 4} p s i = 1. 0 x (3 4. 4) ^ {2} x \frac {4 9 . 9}{6 4 . 4} = 6. 4 p s i
$$

# H.3 CONTRACTION LOSS

Heat exchanger to cold leg. Assume 0.5 velocity head loss for contraction. 16 in Schedule 40, 304 SS pipe.

$$
\mathrm {T} = 1 0 0 0 ^ {\circ} \mathrm {F}
$$

$$
\ell = 5 1. 2 \mathrm {l b} / \mathrm {f t} ^ {3}
$$

$$
A = 1. 2 2 7 f t ^ {2}
$$

$$
V = 4 5, 7 0 0, 0 0 0 \frac {1 b}{h r} x \frac {1}{3 6 0 0} \frac {h r}{\sec} x \frac {1 f t ^ {3}}{5 1 . 2 1 6} x \frac {1}{1 . 2 2 7 x 6 f t ^ {2}} = 3 3. 5 f t / s e c
$$

$$
\Delta P = K V _ {1} ^ {2} \quad \ell = 0. 5 x (3 3. 5) ^ {2} x 5 1. 2 = 3. 1 p s i
$$

H.4 $90^{\circ}$ BEND LOSS

Assume 0.1 velocity head loss per $90^{\circ}$ bend (Ref. 16).

$$
\triangle P = K \frac {v ^ {2}}{2 g} \frac {\ell}{1 4 4} p s i = 0. 1 x \left(\frac {3 3 . 5}{6 4 . 4}\right) ^ {2} x \frac {5 1 . 2}{1 4 4} = . 6 p s i
$$

$$
\mathrm {T} = 1 0 0 ^ {\circ} \mathrm {F}
$$

$$
e = 5 1. 2 \mathrm {l b} / \mathrm {f t} ^ {3}
$$

$$
V = 3 3. 5 f t / s e c
$$

$$
K = 0. 1
$$

H-5 VALVE LOSS

$$
\mathrm {T} = 6 7 5 ^ {\circ} \mathrm {F}
$$

$$
e = 5 3. 9 6 \mathrm {1 b / f t} 3
$$

$$

$$
